Ergo, backed by a decade's worth of [research](documents.md), meticulous testing, and continuous development, has proven up to the challenge. This document will guide you through Ergo's advanced scalability features\n\nThree primary factors are integral to blockchain scalability:\n\n- **Cryptoeconomic incentive model**: This framework ensures that miners are appropriately rewarded for the various costs that come with scaling a blockchain, which can include an increase in state-related costs.\n- **Consensus model**: Depending on the chosen model, certain scalability solutions may or may not be feasible. For instance, the Proof of Stake consensus model does not support Non-interactive Proofs of Proof-of-Work (NiPoPoWs).\n- **Accounting model**: This refers to how transactions and operations are managed within the blockchain. While Bitcoin employs the UTXO Model, Ethereum operates on the Account Model.\n\nThese aspects underpin Ergo's innovative approach to scalability, distinguishing it from other blockchain technologies. Unlike Ethereum's Account model which handles storage changes and validity checks on-chain during code execution, Ergo's [eUTXO](eutxo.md) adopts a different strategy: transactions are created off-chain and validation checks are performed on-chain. \n\nThis system minimizes the operational demand on each node in the network, thereby improving overall efficiency. With the transaction graph's immutability, we can further optimize this process to enhance the number of transactions processed per second. Moreover, the incorporation of light-verifying nodes augments both the scalability and accessibility of the network.\n\nFor a deeper understanding of Ergo's scalability, explore each layer's role in this process:\n\n- [Layer 0](layer0.md): The *Network* or *Peer-to-Peer* Layer\n- [Layer 1](layer1.md): The Core Blockchain Layer\n- [Layer 2](layer2.md): The Off-chain Layer\n\nEach layer works synergistically to bolster Ergo's scalability, making it a versatile and powerful choice for developers and users alike. This cooperative model allows Ergo to offer robust, scalable solutions ready to meet the demands of global expansion.&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;}]" class=""><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; This layer is a critical component because it enables network participants to interact with each other directly without relying on a central authority or intermediary.\n\n\n## Clients\n\n- [Light clients](nipopow_nodes.md): In Ergo, you can get full-node guarantees without storing the full _UTXO set_, enabling a superior bootstrapping experience and improved block validation times.  \n- Quick bootstrapping using [NIPoPoWs](nipopows.md) proofs and UTXO set snapshots in development\n- **[Logarithmic space mining](logspace.md)**  allows for *light miners.* Similar to light clients, light miners can bootstrap with block headers without downloading the entire blockchain. Integrating logarithmic space mining in Ergo is possible via a velvet (soft) fork; see this video from Dionysis Zindros from The University of Athens for a [introduction and their progress so far](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=s05ypkSC7gk).\n\n\n## Bloat\n\n**State Bloat:** One of Ergo's major strengths when scaling is to avoid bloat without compromising functionality. \n\n- Persistent updateable storage is possible, with updates to be checked by a blockchain contract. However, only the digest of the authenticated data structure (and some additional bytes, less than 40) is stored in the UTXO set regardless of the data set size. \n- Ergo utilises a [Storage Rent](rent.md) Fee to prevent spam and recirculate unused data bytes, known as dust. Storage Rent Fee helps clean the network pollution and encourages users to be more active.\n\n\n## Miner-adjusted parameters. \n\n**Block size:** Parameters like block size are not set in stone and can be adjusted by miners. So miners experiencing low full block validation time (as hardware improves with time and software) they may propose or vote to increase the block size. Currently set to `8MB`.\n\n**Transaction size:** As of node `4.0.23`, there is a transaction size limit of `96kb` for the mempool. Larger transactions can only be included manually by miners. \n\nSee the [Governance](governance.md) section for more information.\n\n&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;}]" class=""><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; Ergo blocks have *extension sections* with **mandatory and arbitrary key-value data**; by putting certain anchors there, it is possible to do BitcoinNG-style micro blocks, Aspen-like service chains or generic sidechains with just velvet or soft forks. \n\n## Sharding\n\n**Sharding** as per [*' On the Security and Performance of Blockchain Sharding'*](https://eprint.iacr.org/2021/1276)\n\n## Sub-block confirmation protocols\n\n**Sub-block confirmation protocols**, as seen in [Bitcoin-NG](https://www.usenix.org/system/files/conference/nsdi16/nsdi16-paper-eyal.pdf) and [Flux](https://www.usenix.org/system/files/atc20-li-chenxing.pdf) are an active topic for research in 2023. While the number of transactions a platform can handle is important, on Ergo, the weight of the transactions and the computational cost limit per block are more crucial factors. The cost limit of transactions is affected by various dynamic factors, including the network size and the hardware miners have. \n\nWith the release of [Node v5](jitc.md), the raw TPS numbers should bring us to around **47.5tx/s** - improvements on top of this are still possible. See [this report](https://github.com/ergoplatform/ergo/blob/d3d95e19b37c83b98de13bdf71d6d62b398e8f0d/metrics/Report.ipynb) for technical information on how this is calculated. \n\nTo scale, Ergo must focus on raising the TPS without compromising classic blockchain assumptions and guarantees. \n&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;}]" class=""><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; transform: scale(0.301194, 0.301194); display: none; left: 6676.18px; top: 338.843px;" data-node_json="{&quot;anchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.47760520329750294,&quot;y&quot;:0.016681911722115007},&quot;anchorForce&quot;:0,&quot;mouseAnchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.0004673865940352795,&quot;y&quot;:-0.0008763498638161629},&quot;createdAt&quot;:&quot;2023-05-31T15:12:55.669Z&quot;,&quot;uuid&quot;:&quot;3&quot;,&quot;pos&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.47760520329750294,&quot;y&quot;:0.016681911722115007},&quot;scale&quot;:0.014211473624885458,&quot;intrinsicScale&quot;:1,&quot;vel&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;force&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;followingMouse&quot;:0,&quot;removed&quot;:false,&quot;isResizing&quot;:false,&quot;observer&quot;:{}}" data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;# Executing a Chained Transaction\n\nA chained transaction allows for the sequential use of outputs from off-chain transactions. This guide elaborates on how to submit a chained transaction on Ergo. The basic method illustrated here is derived from the [ergpy examples](https://github.com/mgpai22/ergpy/tree/main/examples). Additional guidance on using the [Transaction Group framework](https://github.com/GetBlok-io/Subpooling#frameworks--abstractions) for managing extensive interlinked transactions is available from GetBlok.\n\n## Establish a Blockchain Connection\n\nStart by defining the URL of the node to connect to the Ergo blockchain. This could be either a MainNet or TestNet node URL. The connection is then established using the `ErgoAppKit`.\n\n```python\n# Assign the node URL\nnode_url: str = \&quot;http://213.239.193.208:9052/\&quot;\n\n# Establish the connection\nergo = appkit.ErgoAppKit(node_url=node_url)\n```\n\n## Set Up Wallet Mnemonic\n\nNext, prepare your wallet mnemonic. This is a sentence-like password that will be used to access your wallet. This example includes receiver addresses and the amount to be sent in each transaction. Furthermore, it sets a sleep time, which is the delay between transaction submissions. The required amount for the genesis outbox, which is the first transaction in the chain, is also calculated.\n\n```python\n# Define the wallet mnemonic\nwallet_mnemonic = \&quot;decline reward asthma enter three clean borrow repeat identify wisdom horn pull entire adapt neglect.\&quot;\n\n# Specify receiver addresses\nreceiver_addresses = [\n    \&quot;3WwdXmYP39DLmDWJ6grH9ArXbWuCt2uGAh46VTfeGPrHKJJY6cSJ\&quot;,\n    \&quot;3WwuG9amNVDwkJdgT5Ce7aJCfeoafVmd9tag9AEiAZwgPi7pYX3w\&quot;,\n    \&quot;3Wxk5oofZ3Laq2CpFW4Fi9YQiaep9bZr6QFg4s4xpzz4bi9tZq2U\&quot;\n]\n\n# Define the amount for each transaction\namount = [0.22, 0.33, 0.11]\n\n# Set the number of consecutive transactions\nconsecutive_transactions = 3\n\n# Define the time gap between submissions of transactions\nsleep_time = 0.5 \n\n# Calculate the total amount for the genesis outbox\ngenesis_amount = [consecutive_transactions * (0.22 + 0.33 + 0.11) + (consecutive_transactions + 1) * 0.001]\n\n# Wallet of the sender\ngenesis_receiver = [\&quot;\&quot;]  \n```\n\n## Generate an Output Box\n\nTo create an output box from the signed transaction, we use the `simple_send` method from the `helper_functions` module. The function returns a signed transaction, which we then use to obtain the genesis outbox.\n\n```python\n# Generate a signed transaction\ngenesis_tx = helper_functions.simple_send(ergo=ergo, amount=genesis_amount, wallet_mnemonic=wallet_mnemonic,\n                                          receiver_addresses=genesis_receiver, return_signed=True)\n# Get the genesis outbox\ngenesis_outbox = appkit.get_outputs_to_spend(genesis_tx, 0)      \n```\n\n## Submit the Transactions\n\nFinally, we submit the transactions to the node. For each transaction in the chain, we use the output box from the previous transaction as the input box for the current transaction. The first transaction retrieves the input box from the genesis outbox. The last transaction is not chained, i.e., its output box\n\n will not be used as an input box in any subsequent transaction. The ID of the genesis transaction and the last transaction are printed to the console. After the final transaction is submitted, the process ends.\n\n```python\n# Print the transaction ID of the genesis transaction\nprint(ergo.txId(genesis_tx))  \n\noutBox_list = []\n\n# Process all the transactions in the chain\nfor x in range(consecutive_transactions):\n    # If it's the first transaction\n    if x == 0: \n        tx_1 = helper_functions.simple_send(ergo=ergo, amount=amount, wallet_mnemonic=wallet_mnemonic,\n                                            receiver_addresses=receiver_addresses, input_box=genesis_outbox,\n                                            return_signed=True, chained=True)\n    # If it's the last transaction\n    elif x == consecutive_transactions - 1: \n        tx_1 = helper_functions.simple_send(ergo=ergo, amount=amount, wallet_mnemonic=wallet_mnemonic,\n                                            receiver_addresses=receiver_addresses, input_box=outBox_list[x - 1],\n                                            return_signed=True)\n    # If it's neither the first nor the last transaction\n    else: \n        tx_1 = helper_functions.simple_send(ergo=ergo, amount=amount, wallet_mnemonic=wallet_mnemonic,\n                                            receiver_addresses=receiver_addresses, input_box=outBox_list[x - 1],\n                                            return_signed=True, chained=True)\n    # Get the outbox from the current transaction\n    tx_1_outbox = appkit.get_outputs_to_spend(tx_1, 0)\n    outBox_list.append(tx_1_outbox)\n    \n    # Pause before processing the next transaction\n    time.sleep(sleep_time)\n\n# Print the transaction ID of the last transaction\nprint(ergo.txId(tx_1)) \n\n# Pause before ending the program\ntime.sleep(sleep_time)\n\n# End the program\nhelper_functions.exit()\n```&quot;}}]"><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; justify-content: space-between; align-items: center;"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewBox="0 0 8 2.125" height="16pt">            <g id="button-delete" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(1 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M 1,1 l 3,0 4,4 4,-4 3,0	     0,3 -4,4 4,4 0,3	     -3,0 -4,-4 -4,4 -3,0	     0,-3 4,-4 -4,-4 0,-3 z" fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-fullscreen" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(21 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M  1, 1 l 3,0 1.5,1.5 -3,0 0,3 -1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15, 1 l -3,0 -1.5,1.5 3,0 0,3 1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15,15 l -3,0 -1.5,-1.5 3,0 0,-3 1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  1,15 l 3,0 1.5,-1.5 -3,0 0,-3 -1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  4, 4 l 2,0 1,1 -2,0 0,2 -1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12, 4 l -2,0 -1,1 2,0 0,2 1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12,12 l -2,0 -1,-1 2,0 0,-2 1,1 0,2 z	     M  4,12 l 2,0 1,-1 -2,0 0,-2 -1,1 0,2 z	     " fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-collapse" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(41 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <circle cx="8" cy="8" r="5.375" stroke="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke-width="2.75" fill="none"></circle>            </g>        </svg><input type="text" value="" class="title-input"></div><div class="content"><textarea class="custom-scrollbar" type="text" size="11" style="background-color: rgb(34, 34, 38); transform: scale(0.301194, 0.301194); display: none; left: 6594.25px; top: 413.238px;" data-node_json="{&quot;anchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.4855507753961028,&quot;y&quot;:0.02389719226753476},&quot;anchorForce&quot;:0,&quot;mouseAnchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0.0006718682289257316,&quot;y&quot;:-0.000759503215307343},&quot;createdAt&quot;:&quot;2023-05-31T15:13:04.186Z&quot;,&quot;uuid&quot;:&quot;5&quot;,&quot;pos&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.4855507753961028,&quot;y&quot;:0.02389719226753476},&quot;scale&quot;:0.014211473624885458,&quot;intrinsicScale&quot;:1,&quot;vel&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;force&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;followingMouse&quot;:0,&quot;removed&quot;:false,&quot;isResizing&quot;:false,&quot;observer&quot;:{}}" data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;# Understanding ZeroJoin\n\nMixers serve as fundamental tools in the process of restoring fungibility for digital tokens. In Ergo, the first such mechanism implemented is known as ZeroJoin. It's constructed on the foundation of ring signatures and a proof of knowledge for a Diffie-Hellman tuple, defined as *(for publicly known g, h, u, v, there exists w, such as g&amp;w == u and h<sup>w</sup> == v)*.\n\nZeroJoin employs two-party interactions called Σ-protocols. Specifically, we require two different types of Σ-protocols explained below.\n\nAssume `G` to be a multiplicative group of prime order `q`, where the Decision Diffie-Hellman (DDH) problem is *hard*.\n\n1. The **first protocol** is labeled as `proveDlog(u)`. It is a proof of knowledge of the Discrete Logarithm of a certain group element `u` in relation to a fixed generator `g`. In other words, the prover validates the knowledge of `x` such that u = g<sup>x</sup>, employing Schnorr signatures.  \n\n2. The **second protocol primitive**, denoted `proveDHTuple(g, h, u, v)`, demonstrates proof of knowledge of a Diffie-Hellman Tuple. Here, the prover verifies the knowledge of `x` such that u = g<sup>x</sup> and v = h<sup>x</sup> for arbitrary generators `g` and `h`.\n\nThis second protocol essentially runs two instances of the first protocol concurrently.\n\nThe protocol proceeds as follows:\n\n1. The prover chooses r from Z<sub>q</sub> at random, calculates (t<sub>0</sub>, t<sub>1</sub>) = (g<sup>r</sup> , h<sup>r</sup>), and forwards (t<sub>0</sub>, t<sub>1</sub>) to the verifier.\n2. The verifier randomly selects c from Z<sub>q</sub> and transfers `c` to the prover.\n3. The prover then sends `z = r + cx` to the verifier. The verifier accepts if g<sup>z</sup> equals t<sub>0</sub> · u<sup>c</sup> and h<sup>z</sup> equals t<sub>1</sub> · v<sup>c</sup>.\n\nFor non-interactive application, we use a variant of the protocol acquired through a Fiat-Shamir transformation. Here, c equals H(t<sub>0</sub>‖t<sub>1</sub>‖m) for a specific message `m` to be signed.\n\nNote that the verification of `proveDHTuple` necessitates four exponentiations, while `proveDlog` requires only two.\n\nBoth protocols are supported by ErgoScript, providing the necessary components for implementing ZeroJoin.\n\n![ZeroJoin Image](../../assets/img/scs/zerojoin.png)\n\nFor additional information on ZeroJoin, please refer to the [ZeroJoin Presentation](https://storage.googleapis.com/ergo-cms-media/docs/CBT_2020_ZeroJoin_Combining_Zerocoin_and_CoinJoin_v3.pdf).&quot;}}]"><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; justify-content: space-between; align-items: center;"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewBox="0 0 8 2.125" height="16pt">            <g id="button-delete" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(1 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M 1,1 l 3,0 4,4 4,-4 3,0	     0,3 -4,4 4,4 0,3	     -3,0 -4,-4 -4,4 -3,0	     0,-3 4,-4 -4,-4 0,-3 z" fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-fullscreen" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(21 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M  1, 1 l 3,0 1.5,1.5 -3,0 0,3 -1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15, 1 l -3,0 -1.5,1.5 3,0 0,3 1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15,15 l -3,0 -1.5,-1.5 3,0 0,-3 1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  1,15 l 3,0 1.5,-1.5 -3,0 0,-3 -1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  4, 4 l 2,0 1,1 -2,0 0,2 -1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12, 4 l -2,0 -1,1 2,0 0,2 1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12,12 l -2,0 -1,-1 2,0 0,-2 1,1 0,2 z	     M  4,12 l 2,0 1,-1 -2,0 0,-2 -1,1 0,2 z	     " fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-collapse" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(41 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <circle cx="8" cy="8" r="5.375" stroke="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke-width="2.75" fill="none"></circle>            </g>        </svg><input type="text" value="" class="title-input"></div><div class="content"><textarea class="custom-scrollbar" type="text" size="11" style="background-color: rgb(34, 34, 38); transform: scale(0.301194, 0.301194); display: none; left: 6695.76px; top: 424.683px;" data-node_json="{&quot;anchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.47570644525923456,&quot;y&quot;:0.025007235428368565},&quot;anchorForce&quot;:0,&quot;mouseAnchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0.0008471382016889684,&quot;y&quot;:-0.0005550215804169048},&quot;createdAt&quot;:&quot;2023-05-31T15:13:11.475Z&quot;,&quot;uuid&quot;:&quot;6&quot;,&quot;pos&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.47570644525923456,&quot;y&quot;:0.025007235428368565},&quot;scale&quot;:0.014211473624885458,&quot;intrinsicScale&quot;:1,&quot;vel&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;force&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;followingMouse&quot;:0,&quot;removed&quot;:false,&quot;isResizing&quot;:false,&quot;observer&quot;:{}}" data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;# Exploring Ergo Box Design\n\n> [Article by Keith Lim](https://keitodot.medium.com/ergo-box-m-f58f444e00d5)\n\n## Understanding the Core Component: Box\n\nAt the heart of a UTXO system are boxes. They serve as essential vessels carrying the value of a currency within a blockchain. The Ergo blockchain enhances this concept by equipping boxes with [registers](registers.md) that are protected by a contract.\n\nRegisters offer the capability to store data and information at specific addresses in the blockchain. Drawing a comparison with everyday items, you might envision these boxes as remote controls, supercharged piggy banks, or simple cups that only allow liquid to be poured in. Taking the cup as an example:\n\n**A Cup:**\n\n- Primarily used to hold liquid\n- Accommodates up to a certain quantity of liquid\n\nThis can be translated into a box-like concept:\n\n**A Cup Box:**\n\n- Designed to store only Ergs or a specific token\n- Allows storage up to a certain limit (enforced by a guard script)\n\nA guard script or contract imposes restrictions on the box. Without this, a box is merely a storage unit for storing information or a certain amount of currency. The incorporation of guard scripts, which set particular rules for the boxes, introduces rigidity to the boxes and shapes their function, much like a remote control. We're less concerned about the internal workings of a remote control (guard script) or the data it transmits (registers and storage), focusing instead on its functionality - pressing a button changes the TV channel.\n\n## What is Box Modeling?\n\nBox modeling provides a structure that assists developers or box designers in crafting a box to perform certain tasks. This framework promotes a structured and straightforward approach to understanding the roles of a box and the mechanics of its operations.\n\n## Designing a Box: Key Principles\n\nA blockchain acts as a financial canvas, providing developers, engineers, and designers the ability to create financial systems atop it. However, any design shortcomings can lead to vulnerabilities to security breaches, unscalable designs, and inefficient processes. Thus, it is crucial to prioritize security, scalability, and efficiency during design.\n\nThree foundational principles for box design:\n\n##### Security\n\nEnsure that the box is not exploitable by unauthorized users.\n\n##### Scalability\n\nDesign the system to handle multiple concurrent requests seamlessly.\n\n#####  Efficiency\n\nA simple design allows engineers to understand and enhance the design effortlessly.\n\n## Steps for Box Modeling\n\nBefore addressing the above principles, understanding the fundamentals of box modeling is essential.\n\nWhile modeling a box, we should ponder:\n\n##### What purpose does this box serve?\n\nA lending box, for example, is designed to streamline the loan process.\n\n##### What data should the box contain?\n\nThe function of the box determines the data stored in it. For instance, a lending box would hold lending-related information in its registers.\n\n##### How will the box execute its function?\n\nThis step is a bit complex as it requires thinking about the overall transaction, not just the box. This stage involves scripting the guard script (or smart contract) to perform its intended function using the data stored in the registers.\n\n### Register Data Types\n\nRegisters can store data in various formats, which can be categorized into single or multiple entries.\n\n**Single data entries include:**\n\n- `Long`\n- `Coll[Byte]` also known as *String*\n- `Bool`\n\n**Multiple data entries include:**\n\n- `Coll[Long]`\n- `Coll[Coll[Byte]]`\n- `Coll[Bool]`\n\nAs we progress, we'll explore how these concepts can be harnessed to develop a replicable framework for designing boxes efficiently, simplifying the potentially overwhelming process into an easy-to-follow guide.&quot;}}]"><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; justify-content: space-between; align-items: center;"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewBox="0 0 8 2.125" height="16pt">            <g id="button-delete" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(1 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M 1,1 l 3,0 4,4 4,-4 3,0	     0,3 -4,4 4,4 0,3	     -3,0 -4,-4 -4,4 -3,0	     0,-3 4,-4 -4,-4 0,-3 z" fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-fullscreen" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(21 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M  1, 1 l 3,0 1.5,1.5 -3,0 0,3 -1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15, 1 l -3,0 -1.5,1.5 3,0 0,3 1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15,15 l -3,0 -1.5,-1.5 3,0 0,-3 1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  1,15 l 3,0 1.5,-1.5 -3,0 0,-3 -1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  4, 4 l 2,0 1,1 -2,0 0,2 -1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12, 4 l -2,0 -1,1 2,0 0,2 1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12,12 l -2,0 -1,-1 2,0 0,-2 1,1 0,2 z	     M  4,12 l 2,0 1,-1 -2,0 0,-2 -1,1 0,2 z	     " fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-collapse" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(41 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <circle cx="8" cy="8" r="5.375" stroke="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke-width="2.75" fill="none"></circle>            </g>        </svg><input type="text" value="" class="title-input"></div><div class="content"><textarea class="custom-scrollbar" type="text" size="11" style="background-color: rgb(34, 34, 38); transform: scale(0.301194, 0.301194); display: none; left: 6768.04px; top: 383.721px;" data-node_json="{&quot;anchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.46869564634870525,&quot;y&quot;:0.021034449379068624},&quot;anchorForce&quot;:0,&quot;mouseAnchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0.0006134449046713009,&quot;y&quot;:-0.0006426565667985232},&quot;createdAt&quot;:&quot;2023-05-31T15:13:17.410Z&quot;,&quot;uuid&quot;:&quot;7&quot;,&quot;pos&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.46869564634870525,&quot;y&quot;:0.021034449379068624},&quot;scale&quot;:0.014211473624885458,&quot;intrinsicScale&quot;:1,&quot;vel&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;force&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;followingMouse&quot;:0,&quot;removed&quot;:false,&quot;isResizing&quot;:false,&quot;observer&quot;:{}}" data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;  ---\n  tags:\n    - Data Model\n  ---\n\n  # Registers\n\n  Each [box](box.md), at the very least, holds four key pieces of information:\n\n  1. The value in NanoErgs (1 Erg = 1000000000 NanoErgs).\n  2. The protection script (akin to Bitcoin's `scriptPubKey`) or \&quot;smart contract\&quot;, safeguarding the box's expenditure.\n  3. Any additional assets or tokens housed within the box.\n  4. Creation details of the box, including the `txId`, which is the ID of the transaction that created the box, and an output index. This information also includes a `maxCreation` height parameter set by the box creator (note: this is not the actual creation height; it aids in the creation of \&quot;payment channels\&quot;).\n\n  These pieces of information are stored in the first four registers of the box. The rest of the registers, from R4 to R9, can be used to store custom data for use in smart contracts. Scripts can access their own registers and the registers of input and output boxes of the spending transaction.\n\n  | Register | Value |\n  |---|---|\n  | R0 | Value (in nanoErgs as Base58) |\n  | R1 | Protection script (Smart Contract) |\n  | R2 | Assets (tokens) |\n  | R3 | Creation details |\n  | R4-R9 | Available for custom use |\n\n  > Remember, registers need to be densely packed; you cannot place an empty register between non-empty ones. \n\n\n  ### Register R0\n\n  This register encapsulates the monetary value of the box in nanoERGs. It can be accessed using the `Box.value` command, where `Box` could signify `SELF` or any box in the `INPUTS` or `OUTPUTS` collections.\n\n  ### Register R1\n\n  Register R1 contains the proposition bytes of the guarding ErgoScript contract associated with the box. Access this register using `Box.propositionBytes`.\n\n  ### Register R2\n\n  R2 holds a collection of tokens stored in the box. Each token is identified by two elements: a unique token id and the quantity of the specific token. Use `Box.tokens` to access this collection.\n\n  ### Register R3\n\n  R3 stores information about the box’s creation, such as the originating transaction id, the box's output index (i.e., the index used in `OUTPUTS`), and the block height at the creation time of the transaction from which the box originates. Access this register using `Box.creationInfo`. The creation height plays a role in Ergo's unique storage rent feature, where boxes can be spent after four years, enabling miners to charge a small fee and recycle ERGs back into the blockchain.\n\n  ### Registers \n\n\n\n  ### Optional Registers R4 - R9\n\n  These registers can contain any data defined when the box first originates from a transaction. The data could be of any common type found in ErgoScript, along with more complex types built from Pairs and Collections. These registers may also contain complex types such as `Box`, `SigmaProp`, `GroupElement`, and `AVLTree`.\n\n  The optional registers can hold any of the following data types:\n\n  - `Int`, `Long` with standard Scala semantics.\n  - `BigInt` - a 256-bit integer (all computations are modulo 2^256).\n  - `GroupElement` - a point on the Secp256k1 curve represented in compressed format.\n  - `Coll[Byte]` - a byte collection, conceptually akin to Scala's `Array[Byte]`.\n  - Collection of the above (i.e., `Coll[Int]`, `Coll[GroupElement]`, `Coll[Coll[Byte]]`, and so forth).\n\n  A `boxId` is calculated based on the contents of all the registers, uniquely defining a box. This can be equated to Bitcoin's (txId, vOut) pairs.\n\n  > Note that Ergo `txId` is dependent solely on the message and not on signatures (similar to Bitcoin SegWit transactions). Hence, a txId is accessible even before signing. Like Bitcoin, Ergo supports chained transactions, meaning boxes with 0 confirmations can be spent.\n\n\n\n\n  ## Typed\n\n  Both [ErgoScript](/dev/scs/ergoscript) and [ErgoTree](/dev/scs/ergotree) are **typed**, meaning that when a script accesses a register, it anticipates a specific type which is denoted in brackets.\n\n  For instance,\n\n  ```scala\n  // Assign the value of the R4 register of the current box to the variable x\n  val x = SELF.R4[Int]\n  ```\n\n  In the above example, the register is expected to have an `Int` type. Therefore, the expression `SELF.R4[Int]` should return a valid `Option[Int]` type value.\n\n  When you try to retrieve the value of the register `SELF.R4[Int]`, there are three potential scenarios:\n\n  1. The register does not exist, hence `SELF.R4[Int].isDefined` will return `false`.\n  2. The register has an `Int` type value, thus `SELF.R4[Int].get` will fetch that value, and `SELF.R4[Int].isDefined` will be `true`.\n  3. The register carries a value of a different type, in which case `SELF.R4[Int]` will fail, and it will not produce a valid `Option[Int]` type value.\n\n  In some use cases, a register may contain values of various types. An additional register can be employed as a tag to facilitate the access of such a register.\n\n  ```scala\n  val tagOpt = SELF.R5[Int] // Retrieve the value of the register R5 of type Int and assign it to the variable `tagOpt`\n  val res = if (tagOpt.isDefined) { // Check if `tagOpt` is not empty\n    val tag = tagOpt.get // Obtain the value of `tagOpt` and assign it to the variable `tag`\n    if (tag == 1) { // Check if `tag` equals 1\n      val x = SELF.R4[Int].get // Retrieve the value of the register R4 of type Int and assign it to the variable `x`\n      // Compute `res` using the value `x` of type Int\n    } else if (tag == 2) { // Check if `tag` equals 2\n      val x = SELF.R4[GroupElement].get // Retrieve the value of the register R4 of type GroupElement and assign it to the variable `x`\n      // Compute `res` using the value `x` of type GroupElement\n    } else if (tag == 3) { // Check if `tag` equals 3\n      val x = SELF.R4[ Array[Byte] ].get // Retrieve the value of the register R4 of type Array[Byte] and assign it to the variable `x`\n      // Compute `res` using the value `x` of type Array[Byte]\n    } else {\n      // Compute `res` when `tag` is neither 1, 2, nor 3\n    }\n  } else {\n    // Compute `res` when the register is not present\n  }\n  ```\n&quot;}}]"><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; justify-content: space-between; align-items: center;"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewBox="0 0 8 2.125" height="16pt">            <g id="button-delete" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(1 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M 1,1 l 3,0 4,4 4,-4 3,0	     0,3 -4,4 4,4 0,3	     -3,0 -4,-4 -4,4 -3,0	     0,-3 4,-4 -4,-4 0,-3 z" fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-fullscreen" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(21 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M  1, 1 l 3,0 1.5,1.5 -3,0 0,3 -1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15, 1 l -3,0 -1.5,1.5 3,0 0,3 1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15,15 l -3,0 -1.5,-1.5 3,0 0,-3 1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  1,15 l 3,0 1.5,-1.5 -3,0 0,-3 -1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  4, 4 l 2,0 1,1 -2,0 0,2 -1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12, 4 l -2,0 -1,1 2,0 0,2 1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12,12 l -2,0 -1,-1 2,0 0,-2 1,1 0,2 z	     M  4,12 l 2,0 1,-1 -2,0 0,-2 -1,1 0,2 z	     " fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-collapse" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(41 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <circle cx="8" cy="8" r="5.375" stroke="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke-width="2.75" fill="none"></circle>            </g>        </svg><input type="text" value="" class="title-input"></div><div class="content"><textarea class="custom-scrollbar" type="text" size="11" style="background-color: rgb(34, 34, 38); color: rgb(187, 187, 187); overflow-y: auto; resize: both; width: 218px; height: 0px;"></textarea></div><div class="resize-container"><div class="resize-handle"></div></div></div></div><div data-init="window" data-uuid="8" style="position: absolute; transform: scale(0.0713613, 0.0713613); display: none; left: 6757.33px; top: 291.726px;" data-node_json="{&quot;anchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.46973457841634775,&quot;y&quot;:0.012112202905409063},&quot;anchorForce&quot;:0,&quot;mouseAnchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0.0001038158045276738,&quot;y&quot;:-0.00011073685816285171},&quot;createdAt&quot;:&quot;2023-05-31T15:13:25.124Z&quot;,&quot;uuid&quot;:&quot;8&quot;,&quot;pos&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.46973457841634775,&quot;y&quot;:0.012112202905409063},&quot;scale&quot;:0.0033670925935141995,&quot;intrinsicScale&quot;:1,&quot;vel&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;force&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;followingMouse&quot;:0,&quot;removed&quot;:false,&quot;isResizing&quot;:false,&quot;observer&quot;:{}}" data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;## Standards for Asset Issuance\n\nErgo treats custom tokens as first-class citizens. A particular [register](registers.md) (`R2`) within a [box](box.md) holds pairs of `tokenId` and `amount`. There's a hard limit on the number of tokens per box or transaction, which is quite generous, allowing up to **255** secondary tokens in a transaction or a box. However, there are indirect restrictions too: a box can't exceed **4** kilobytes, and the addition of tokens also increases the computational cost estimation of the transaction.\n\nThe amount of Erg is specified directly in the register `R0`, written as a number without any identifier. \n\nHowever, Ergs differ from other tokens in the following significant ways:\n\n* **ERGs cannot be burnt**: the total amount of ergs in transaction inputs must equal the total amount in the outputs. Other tokens, unlike **ERGs**, can be burnt: the total amount of a token in transaction inputs must be no less than the amount in the outputs.\n* [Storage rent](rent.md) is **payable in ERG only**.\n\nTokens can be representative of a variety of things, like shares, units of complementary currency, or any other conceivable item.\n\n### Singletons\n\nIn the UTXO model, a token issued with a quantity of exactly one unit is referred to as a `singleton` token. Singleton tokens can be used to emulate long-lived accounts similar to those found in Waves, Ethereum Classic, etc. A transaction that spends an old box with the singleton token and creates a new one can be controlled by the script of the old box, requiring the new box to have certain characteristics (such as a specific script or amount). This allows a smart account, marked with the token, to exist and alter its state as dictated by the smart account contract through a chain of transactions.\n\nOne specific example of a singular token is an [oracle](oracles.md). For instance, a token can be created to act as an ERG/EUR exchange rates oracle. A box that contains this token would encode the exchange rate in a specific register. Since the oracle is a long-lived account, contracts can know the oracle token identifier beforehand and refer to it.\n\n**How can new assets be created?**\n\nThere is an exception to the rule of weak preservation *(the total amount in inputs is no less than the total amount in outputs)*: a transaction can create tokens from thin air in its outputs if the asset identifier matches the identifier of the transaction's first input box. Since the box identifier is cryptographically unique, it's impossible to have a second asset with the same identifier, assuming the hash function used is collision-resistant (which it indeed is). This rule also implies that only one new asset can be created per transaction.\n\nThe Ergo reference implementation wallet employs specific [registers](registers.md) in a particular manner, though the protocol doesn't mandate this: \n\n* `R4` - verbose name\n* `R5` - description\n* `R6` - number of decimal places\n* Additional registers (`R7`, `R8`, `R9`) can hold asset-specific information\n\nApplications can follow this convention, but the protocol does not enforce it.&quot;}}]"><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; justify-content: space-between; align-items: center;"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewBox="0 0 8 2.125" height="16pt">            <g id="button-delete" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(1 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M 1,1 l 3,0 4,4 4,-4 3,0	     0,3 -4,4 4,4 0,3	     -3,0 -4,-4 -4,4 -3,0	     0,-3 4,-4 -4,-4 0,-3 z" fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-fullscreen" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(21 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M  1, 1 l 3,0 1.5,1.5 -3,0 0,3 -1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15, 1 l -3,0 -1.5,1.5 3,0 0,3 1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15,15 l -3,0 -1.5,-1.5 3,0 0,-3 1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  1,15 l 3,0 1.5,-1.5 -3,0 0,-3 -1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  4, 4 l 2,0 1,1 -2,0 0,2 -1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12, 4 l -2,0 -1,1 2,0 0,2 1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12,12 l -2,0 -1,-1 2,0 0,-2 1,1 0,2 z	     M  4,12 l 2,0 1,-1 -2,0 0,-2 -1,1 0,2 z	     " fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-collapse" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(41 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <circle cx="8" cy="8" r="5.375" stroke="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke-width="2.75" fill="none"></circle>            </g>        </svg><input type="text" value="" class="title-input"></div><div class="content"><textarea class="custom-scrollbar" type="text" size="11" style="background-color: rgb(34, 34, 38); transform: scale(0.0713613, 0.0713613); display: none; left: 6785.87px; top: 305.927px;" data-node_json="{&quot;anchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.4669661569622765,&quot;y&quot;:0.013489492578809527},&quot;anchorForce&quot;:0,&quot;mouseAnchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0.00029068425267753106,&quot;y&quot;:-0.0001730263408794546},&quot;createdAt&quot;:&quot;2023-05-31T15:13:30.278Z&quot;,&quot;uuid&quot;:&quot;9&quot;,&quot;pos&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.4669661569622765,&quot;y&quot;:0.013489492578809527},&quot;scale&quot;:0.0033670925935141995,&quot;intrinsicScale&quot;:1,&quot;vel&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;force&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;followingMouse&quot;:0,&quot;removed&quot;:false,&quot;isResizing&quot;:false,&quot;observer&quot;:{}}" data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;# Understanding Blocks in Ergo\n\nErgo operates with a block interval [set at two minutes](difficulty.md). Each block initially releases 75 Ergs, which are distributed among the miners and the Treasury. This arrangement is applicable for the first two years of operation. Starting from the second year, the release rate undergoes a decrease by 3.0 Ergs, followed by a consistent reduction of an additional 3.0 Ergs every three months. This systematic decrease was initially programmed to halt the emission eight years after Ergo's launch. However, following the introduction of [EIP-27](eip27.md), the emission period has been extended up until approximately the year 2045.\n\n## Understanding the Block Structure: The Extension Section\n\nSimilar to other blockchains such as Bitcoin and Ethereum, Ergo divides blocks into different sections for better functionality. While Bitcoin comprises simply a block header and transactions, Ergo offers a more complex structure with additional sections:\n\n* Header\n* Transactions\n* Extensions\n* Proofs of UTXO transformation\n\nAmong these, the 'extension' section carries specific obligatory fields. These include NIPoPoWs links (appearing once every 1,024 block epoch) and parameters that allow for [miner voting](governance.md), such as the current block size. The extension section can also accommodate arbitrary fields as per requirement.\n\nThe essence of this intricate design is to allow diverse nodes and clients to download only those block sections which are relevant to them. This strategy significantly curtails the storage, bandwidth, and CPU usage demands, making the system more efficient.\n\n## Useful Resources\n\nErgo further bolsters its flexibility and efficiency by supporting [Superblock Clients](log_space.md). This feature offers an additional layer of adaptability to meet varying user requirements.\n&quot;}}]"><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; justify-content: space-between; align-items: center;"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewBox="0 0 8 2.125" height="16pt">            <g id="button-delete" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(1 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M 1,1 l 3,0 4,4 4,-4 3,0	     0,3 -4,4 4,4 0,3	     -3,0 -4,-4 -4,4 -3,0	     0,-3 4,-4 -4,-4 0,-3 z" fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-fullscreen" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(21 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M  1, 1 l 3,0 1.5,1.5 -3,0 0,3 -1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15, 1 l -3,0 -1.5,1.5 3,0 0,3 1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15,15 l -3,0 -1.5,-1.5 3,0 0,-3 1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  1,15 l 3,0 1.5,-1.5 -3,0 0,-3 -1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  4, 4 l 2,0 1,1 -2,0 0,2 -1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12, 4 l -2,0 -1,1 2,0 0,2 1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12,12 l -2,0 -1,-1 2,0 0,-2 1,1 0,2 z	     M  4,12 l 2,0 1,-1 -2,0 0,-2 -1,1 0,2 z	     " fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-collapse" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(41 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <circle cx="8" cy="8" r="5.375" stroke="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke-width="2.75" fill="none"></circle>            </g>        </svg><input type="text" value="" class="title-input"></div><div class="content"><textarea class="custom-scrollbar" type="text" size="11" style="background-color: rgb(34, 34, 38); color: rgb(187, 187, 187); overflow-y: auto; resize: both; width: 218px; height: 0px;"></textarea></div><div class="resize-container"><div class="resize-handle"></div></div></div></div><div data-init="window" data-uuid="10" style="position: absolute; transform: scale(0.0713613, 0.0713613); display: none; left: 6781.52px; top: 277.453px;" data-node_json="{&quot;anchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.46738834123402234,&quot;y&quot;:0.010727992178373419},&quot;anchorForce&quot;:0,&quot;mouseAnchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0.00021455266269049256,&quot;y&quot;:-0.00013842107270356334},&quot;createdAt&quot;:&quot;2023-05-31T15:13:35.143Z&quot;,&quot;uuid&quot;:&quot;10&quot;,&quot;pos&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.46738834123402234,&quot;y&quot;:0.010727992178373419},&quot;scale&quot;:0.0033670925935141995,&quot;intrinsicScale&quot;:1,&quot;vel&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;force&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;followingMouse&quot;:0,&quot;removed&quot;:false,&quot;isResizing&quot;:false,&quot;observer&quot;:{}}" data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;---\ntags:\n  - Data Model\n  - Box\n---\n\n# The Ergo 'Box' model\n\nThe Ergo platform employs a transactional model akin to Bitcoin, known as the Unspent Transaction Output (UTxO) model. Here, transactions expend and generate single-use entities, referred to as a ***'box'***. \n\nIn [ErgoScript](ergoscript.md), a 'box' is akin to a more versatile version of what a UTXO (Unspent Transaction Output) represents in Bitcoin and many other cryptocurrencies. A box is not only a ledger entry denoting the amount of cryptocurrency owned by a particular address, but it also carries 'registers', allowing it to contain additional data. This data could range from simple values to more complex structures, which can later be integrated into transactions and used in the execution of smart contracts.\n\nThis makes Ergo's box different from a traditional UTXO, which only represents an amount of unspent cryptocurrency associated with a certain address. In UTXO-based cryptocurrencies, each transaction consumes one or more UTXOs as inputs and creates one or more UTXOs as outputs, with the 'unspent' outputs being the 'coins' that can be spent in future transactions.\n\nThe term 'box' in Ergo's context captures the idea that these entities are like containers holding various types of information (value, tokens, custom data, etc.), beyond just the unspent transaction output balance. This makes the boxes in Ergo significantly more flexible and functional, enabling more complex operations, such as running scripts or smart contracts, directly on the blockchain.\n\n\n\n## Key Points\n\n- A box is an immutable unit, which can be created or removed, but never altered. \n- The box is not just a simple coin; it houses data, code, and registers, with all of its contents exclusively stored in the registers. \n- Four pre-defined registers contain the box's monetary value, its protection script, and the ID of the transaction that created the box.\n- Each box has a unique ID, derived from the unique contents of the box, including the data of the transaction that created it.\n- Boxes are integral to the Ergo protocol. The active box set is authenticated through a hash-based data structure, facilitating the development of lightweight full nodes, as detailed in [this paper](https://eprint.iacr.org/2016/994). \n- A box can hold up to six additional [registers](registers.md) with typed data, accessible by the script.\n- Transactions consist of both *input* and *output* boxes. \n\n\n\n## Additional Box Functions\n\nBesides the registers, each box features a unique identification hash that can be referenced using the `id` function. Box ids are computed by applying the `blake2b256` hash function to the box's content, expressed as a `Coll[Byte]`. You can directly access the un-hashed byte collection representing a box using the `bytes` function. Note that each box’s content and id are cryptographically unique, meaning that no two boxes within the blockchain can share the same id or content bytes. This uniqueness is guaranteed by the inclusion of `creationInfo` in each box, as transaction ids and associated output indexes must be unique to a given UTXO. The `bytesWithoutRef` function can be used to retrieve a `Coll[Byte]` that excludes such information.\n\n## Example\n\n```scala\n{\n\t// Retrieve the value and token multipliers from the registers of the current box\n\tval valueMultiplier = SELF.R4[Int].get\n\tval tokenMultiplier = INPUTS(1).R4[Int].get\n\n\t// Check if the current box is the same as the first input box\n\tif(SELF.id == INPUTS(0).id){\n\t\t// If it is, check if the output box has the correct value and token amounts\n\t\tval outputValue = OUTPUTS(0).value == SELF.value * valueMultiplier \n\t\tval outputTokens = OUTPUTS(0).tokens(0)._2 == SELF.value * tokenMultiplier \n\t\t// Return a Sigma proposition that is true only if both outputValue and outputTokens are true\n\t\tsigmaProp(outputValue &amp;&amp; outputTokens)\n\t}else{\n\t\t// If the current box is not the same as the first input box, check if the output goes to a specified address\n\t\tval outputGoesToCheese = {\n\t\t\t// Create a public key that corresponds to a specific address\n\t\t\tPK(\&quot;9etXmP7D3ZkWssDopWcWkCPpjn22RVuEyXoFSbVPWAvvzDbcDXE\&quot;).propBytes\n\t\t\t\t== OUTPUTS(0).propositionBytes\n\t\t}\n\t\t// Return a Sigma proposition that is true only if outputGoesToCheese is true\n\t\tsigmaProp(outputGoesToCheese)\n\t}\n}\n```\n\n## Example \n\nConsider the 'proof-of-no-premine' from the Ergo genesis state. This box contains the last block IDs from Bitcoin and Ethereum at the launch time, as well as the latest news headlines:\n\n```JSON\n     {\n    \&quot;boxId\&quot;: \&quot;assets\&quot;: [],\n    \&quot;creationHeight\&quot;: 0,\n    \&quot;additionalRegisters\&quot;: {\n      \&quot;R5\&quot;: \&quot;0e464272657869743a20626f746820546f727920736964657320706c617920646f776e207269736b206f66206e6f2d6465616c20616674657220627573696e65737320616c61726d\&quot;,\n      \&quot;R8\&quot;: \&quot;0e45d094d0b8d0b2d0b8d0b4d0b5d0bdd0b4d18b20d0a7d0a2d09fd09720d0b2d18bd180d0b0d181d182d183d18220d0bdd0b02033332520d0bdd0b020d0b0d0bad186d0b8d18e\&quot;,\n      \&quot;R7\&quot;: \&quot;0e54e8bfb0e8af84efbc9ae5b9b3e8a1a1e38081e68c81e7bbade38081e58c85e5aeb9e28094e28094e696b0e697b6e4bba3e5ba94e5afb9e585a8e79083e58c96e68c91e68898e79a84e4b8ade59bbde4b98be98193\&quot;,\n data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;---\ntags:\n  - Tutorial\n---\n\n# Ergo Platform Blockchain Integration Guide\n\nThis guide facilitates developers in integrating the Ergo Blockchain Platform. For suggestions on improvements, please contact us at team@ergoplatform.org or join our [`#development` on Discord](https://discord.gg/kj7s7nb).\n\n## Getting Started\n\nHere's a brief overview of key features of Ergo:\n\n* Transactions in Ergo, akin to Bitcoin, involve multiple *inputs* and *outputs*. Unspent outputs are used once, termed as **single-use entities**. Although built from scratch, Ergo's scripts and transaction formats differ from Bitcoin's.\n* Ergo contains standard scripts, associated with addresses like `P2PK` addresses, similar to Bitcoin. [Learn more about the address scheme here.](/dev/wallet/address)\n* An ergo eutxo box stores [registers](registers.md) to hold arbitrary values, such as its native tokens, instead of a single amount (like BTC). Thus, each box contains an ERG amount and may include {tokenid, token amount} pairs, all following the UTXO model.\n* Ergo's inbuilt wallet API suffices for most use cases. The API uses a Swagger interface on `127.0.0.1:9053` by default in the mainnet (`9052` on the testnet).\n* A comprehensive guide on [setting up a node](install.md) and a dedicated [troubleshooting page](troubleshooting.md) are available.\n\n### Node Wallet\n\n- **Web interface**: Access the web interface at [127.0.0.1:9053/panel](https://127.0.0.1:9053/panel) (`9052` on the testnet). \n\nMajor functionalities include:\n\n* Wallet creation (`/wallet/init`) and mnemonic generation\n* Wallet restoration (`/wallet/restore`) from mnemonic\n* Wallet unlock (`/wallet/unlock`) for transaction signing\n* Wallet lock (`/wallet/lock`)\n* Sending a simple payment (`/wallet/payment/send`)\n* Checking wallet status (`/wallet/status`)\n* Deriving a new key according to EIP-3 (BIP 44 implementation for Ergo) (`/wallet/deriveNextKey`)\n* Checking wallet balance (`/wallet/balances`) for all addresses\n* Retrieving wallet transactions (`/wallet/transactions`) for all addresses \n\n### Creating an External Wallet\n\nYou can develop your wallet logic externally using one of the available libraries and the block explorer. \n\n**Please ensure to take into consideration mempool transactions to avoid double-spending.**\n\nAvailable libraries include:\n\n* [*ergo-appkit*](https://github.com/ergoplatform/ergo-appkit): A complete Java SDK.\n* [*ergo-wallet*](https://mvnrepository.com/artifact/org.ergoplatform/ergo-wallet): Developed in Java, the source code is available at the [Ergo node repository](https://github.com/ergoplatform/ergo/tree/master/ergo-wallet).\n* [*sigma-rust*](https://github.com/ergoplatform/sigma-rust/): A Rust library with WASM bindings for JavaScript/TypeScript.\n* [*ergo-ts*](https://github.com/coinbarn/ergo-ts): Developed in TypeScript.\n* [*ergo-golang*](https://github.com/azhiganov/ergo-golang): This is a Go library, still in early development stages.\n\n\n### Offline Signing\n\n- Transaction assembly and offline signing demo using ergo-wallet and Java is provided in [AdressGenerationDemo.java](https://gist.github.com/kushti/c040f244865a451b94df01032c7a3456 )\n- Transaction assembly and signing in Rust: [tx_builder.rs](https://github.com/ergoplatform/sigma-rust/blob/d70bea875792c4e383bfdd71754338695bdb37f8/ergo-lib/src/wallet/tx_builder.rs#L552-L592) and [signing.rs](https://github.com/ergoplatform/sigma-rust/blob/d70bea875792c4e383bfdd71754338695bdb37f8/ergo-lib/src/wallet/signing.rs#L133-L161)\n- [Transaction assembly and signing in JavaScript](https://github.com/ergoplatform/sigma-rust/blob/d70bea875792c4e383bfdd71754338695bdb37f8/bindings/ergo-lib-wasm/tests/test_transaction.js#L9-L69)\n\n\n### Address generation\n\nSecret seed and derived addresses generation demo using ergo-wallet and Java is provided in [AdressGenerationDemo.java](https://gist.github.com/kushti/70dcfa841dfb504721f09c911b0fc53d)\n\n\nYou can use the `/wallet/deriveNextKey` API to generate new addresses in the same wallet.\n\n```bash\ncurl -X GET \&quot;http://localhost:9053/wallet/deriveNextKey\&quot; -H  \&quot;accept: application/json\&quot; -H  \&quot;api_key: hello\&quot;\n```\n\n### Address validation\n\nFor an exchange, you can restrict people to only withdraw to P2PK addresses and invalidate any other address. Supporting other types is not recommended. See [address.md] for more information on the types of addresses. \n\n[ergo-simple-addresses](https://github.com/kushti/ergo-simple-addresses) contains a few zero-dependencies Java-friendly utils for working with addresses.\n\n\n\n\n### Composing transactions outside the node\n\n\nTo get unspent UTXOs for some address, please use the `transactions/boxes/byAddress/unspent` Explorer API method: \n\n```bash\nhttps://api.ergoplatform.com/transactions/boxes/byAddress/unspent/9gAE5e454UT5s3NB1625u1LynQYPS2XzzBEK4xumvSZdqnXT35M \n```\n\nIt would be best if you excluded UTXOs spent in the mempool. Use the`/transactions/unconfirmed/byAddress` Explorer API method for that:\n\n```bash\nhttps://api.ergoplatform.com/transactions/unconfirmed/byAddress/9gAE5e454UT5s3NB1625u1LynQYPS2XzzBEK4xumvSZdqnXT35M\n```\n\n### Broadcasting transaction\n\n\nTo broadcast a transaction made outside the node, the easiest way is to serialize it into `JSON`; in Java, it could be like this:\n\n```Java\nJson json = JsonCodecsWrapper.ergoLikeTransactionEncoder().apply(tx);\nSystem.out.println(json.toString());\n```\n\nAnd then send this `JSON` via a POST request to the public Explorer. \n\n```bash\nhttps://api.ergoplatform.com/api/v0/transactions/send*\n```\n\nyour private Explorer or a node with open API (`POST` to `http://{node_ip}:9053/transactions` )\n\n\n\n## Infrastructure\n\n### Explorer\n\nThere is a public explorer hosted at [explorer.ergoplatform.com](https://explorer.ergoplatform.com/)\n\nYou can use [*ergo-bootstrap*](https://github.com/ergoplatform/ergo-bootstrap) to easily install the Explorer backend (and not rely on public ones). \n\n### Testnet\n\nSee [this page](/node/testnet) for information on the public testnets.\n\n\n\n## Troubleshooting\n\n### Dust Collection\n\nPlease collect dust from miners' deposits periodically (which creates many small UTXOs).\n\n\n\nErgo is based on the extended-UTXO model. A side-effect of UTXOs is a term coined *dust*. Dust refers to fractional values of ERG and is usually below the protocol fee; miner wallets are prone to becoming *dusty* with the stream of rewards coming into their wallet. All these excess UTXOs can cause a slowdown and ultimately impact your node functionality. **This is important to set up for big exchanges that will receive a lot of mining traffic. You must collect dust aggressively; new dust arrives all the time for miners**\n\n\n\n**To solve**\n\n- Get utxos from `/wallet/boxes/unspent` with min number of confirmations\n- Get their `ids` and `total sum`\n- Get their *binary representations* of utxos via `/utxo/byIdBinary/{boxId}`\n\n\nFinally, construct the payment transaction like this:\n  \n```JSON\n{\n  \&quot;requests\&quot;: [\n    {\n      \&quot;address\&quot;: \&quot;3WwbzW6u8hKWBcL1W7kNVMr25s2UHfSBnYtwSHvrRQt7DdPuoXrt\&quot;,\n      \&quot;value\&quot;: 10000000000\n    }\n  ],\n  \&quot;fee\&quot;: 1000000,\n  \&quot;inputsRaw\&quot;: [\n    \&quot;utxo1\&quot;, \&quot;utxo2\&quot;\n  ],\n  \&quot;dataInputsRaw\&quot;: [\n  ]\n} \n```\n\nAnd post to `/wallet/transaction/send`.\n\nSet value and fee accordingly, `value + fee = total sum of utxos`\n\nYou can query with specific parameters like this, \n\n```bash\ncurl -X GET \&quot;http://127.0.0.1:9053/wallet/boxes/unspent?minConfirmations=10&amp;minInclusionHeight=0\&quot; -H  \&quot;accept: application/json\&quot; -H  \&quot;api_key: hello\&quot;\n```\n\nAnother (and simple) way to collect is to set \n\n```conf\nergo {\n wallet {\n   maxInputs = 300 \n   optimalInputs = 100\n }\n}\n```\n\nAnd send `1 ERG` to the change address; however, the node will attach 100 dust inputs (so send any large amounts of change to the change address as well)\n\n#### Failed to sign boxes\n\nThis error can occur due to too many inputs collected in a transaction for dusty wallets. \n\n```bash\nFailed to sign boxes due to Estimated execution cost 1001580 exceeds the limit 1000000: Vector(ErgoBox(0275eb3a125bc02fe997cb98c0de8131bd9b2e4617110d\n```\n\n\n\n### Native Assets\n\nIn the case of large airdrops, many users mistakenly end up putting exchange addresses to receive native assets. An *auto-burn* method will be in future versions of the node to reduce the manual component of this task. See this [Issue](https://github.com/ergoplatform/ergo/issues/1604) for more information.\n\nSend the following request via `/wallet/payment/send`, replacing the `tokenId` with the IDs from the tokens spamming your wallets. \n\n```JSON\n[\n  {\n    \&quot;address\&quot;: \&quot;4MQyMKvMbnCJG3aJ\&quot;,\n    \&quot;value\&quot;: 100000000,\n    \&quot;assets\&quot;: [\n      {\&quot;tokenId\&quot;:\&quot;e55adbda4e42f2bd21b1cb9498c105ff3bc1069012942d6158412f55759369c3\&quot;,\&quot;amount\&quot;:1},\n      {\&quot;tokenId\&quot;:\&quot;6dd3e0ac4edd9702094aa4e3cad7c0c73d5437292c11805adf5d5068312748b4\&quot;,\&quot;amount\&quot;:1}\n    ]\n  }\n]\n```\n\n#### Tokens with Value\n\n- SigUSD ($1): `472c3d4ecaa08fb7392ff041ee2e6af75f4a558810a74b28600549d5392810e8` \n- SigRSV\n- ergopad\n- NETA\n- LunaDog\n- Erdoge\n\n### FAQ\n\n**Are P2S and P2SH two address formats for the same script?**\n\nIt can be. So you can create a p2s and a p2sh address from the same script. In p2s, the script is serialized into the Address; in p2sh, you only have a hash of that serialized script.\n\n**Will there be any problems with supporting addresses other than P2PK?**\n\nNo problem if the user knows what he is doing, which is not true in many cases. It also adds more complexity. Especially for p2s Addresses since you cannot validate the input size in your form. \n\n**ergoTree<->address related conversions**\n\nWhen you use appkit, Address.create() takes the address string. You can get the ergotree from the resulting object.\n\n**Some transactions don't seem to pay fees?**\n\nFees are not part of the core protocol, but if you miss them, the transaction will not be propagated around the network by default.\n\n**What is the generation algorithm of boxid?**\n\nIt is a hash of the box contents.\n\n[See the code in AppKit](https://github.com/ergoplatform/ergo-appkit/blob/9e19c13d82966eaee59433d16c4fb987bea363a7/lib-impl/src/main/java/org/ergoplatform/appkit/impl/OutBoxBuilderImpl.scala#L66)\n\nBytes are unique as the box contains\n- The `id` of the parent transaction, \n- the output position in the transaction\n- a unique transaction id. \n\n&quot;}}]"><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; transform: scale(0.0713613, 0.0713613); display: none; left: 6748.69px; top: 311.421px;" data-node_json="{&quot;anchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.4705720259062043,&quot;y&quot;:0.014022413708718248},&quot;anchorForce&quot;:0,&quot;mouseAnchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0.00015226317997391048,&quot;y&quot;:-0.00015226317997391915},&quot;createdAt&quot;:&quot;2023-05-31T15:13:55.199Z&quot;,&quot;uuid&quot;:&quot;12&quot;,&quot;pos&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.4705720259062043,&quot;y&quot;:0.014022413708718248},&quot;scale&quot;:0.0033670925935141995,&quot;intrinsicScale&quot;:1,&quot;vel&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;force&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;followingMouse&quot;:0,&quot;removed&quot;:false,&quot;isResizing&quot;:false,&quot;observer&quot;:{}}" data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;---\ntags:\n  - NIPoPoWs\n  - Mining\n---\n\n# Log-Space Mining\n\n\nMiners must constantly maintain the blockchain, whether it is Ergo, Bitcoin, or another PoW consensus model. In addition to using computational resources, miners also use storage resources that maintain all blockchain data from the genesis block.\n\nA new miner's problem: Is downloading all the data from the genesis block strictly necessary? Why is it not possible to download only the most relevant blocks to maintain the network?\n\nThe block headers of the blockchain should be enough to access the necessary data. [NIPoPoWs](https://NIPoPoWs.com/) (Non-Interactive Proofs of Proof of Work) can be integrated to form interlinked block header sets that will reduce historical data storage.\n\nWhen needing to access key blocks in the blockchain, miners should be able to do this from the headers of the old blocks efficiently. That is because each new block must indicate all of the current networks. As new blocks are created, a set of new block headers can be enough to check for the current UTXO set. Since the new blocks contain the data of old stringed block header sets, it enables light mining by eliminating the need to download all the blockchain data.\n\nWhat are we trying to optimise by stringing old PoW history and compiling it into a snapshot?\n\nIf we say **C=old** blocks and **K=new** blocks, then included blocks in the snapshot can be growing when **K=new** blocks are constant, and C=old blocks are linear. But it can also be shrinking depending on the smart contract applications. The problem of maintaining heavy loads of data by the miners can be solved by bootstrapping through NIPoPoWs. \n\n\n**NIPoPoW Implementation**\n\nInstead of accessing all of the blocks, superblocks (or light clients) are enough to verify all of the blocks. This is accomplished by maintaining the historical data of the blockchain through smart contracts. The introduction of these superblock clients on NIPoPoWs can be done via 'velvet' or soft forks, and after that *\&quot;light\&quot;* miners can bootstrap through \&quot;online\&quot; mining.\n\nNIPoPoWs enable smart contracts to maintain historical data so that new *\&quot;light\&quot;* miners can work in a so-called \&quot;online\&quot; fashion. This is the main idea of Logarithmic Space Mining. Instead of saving all the blockchain data locally on nodes, the unnecessary part can be compiled into the blockchain itself. New miners do not need to carry the historical data, and as they continue to mine, new *\&quot;light\&quot;* miners will help other *\&quot;light\&quot;* miners bootstrap. There will be no need to carry old historical data, and old miners can abandon historical data for lighter mining. This is how the miner population can abandon old blocks and make the system much more efficient.\n\n## Resources\n\nIn [NIPoPoWs &amp; Log-Space Mining – Ergo Cast Episode #5](https://ergocast.io/episode/NIPoPoWs-ergo-cast-episode-5/), Dionysis Zindros explains the technical landscape of Non-Interactive Proofs of Proof-of-Work. Dionysis takes a diligent approach for the Ergo Cast with a detailed rundown of what Non-Interactive Proofs of Proof-of-Work truly are. Furthermore, Dionysis evaluates the operation, implementation, and impact that his primitive delivers upon. Furthermore, we unveil a brand-new piece of research that -as of yet- has never been shared publicly: log-space mining.\n\nThis section is based on a [recently published](https://eprint.iacr.org/2021/623.pdf) article by IOHK. For an additional resource, please see the following [video.](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=s05ypkSC7gk)\n&quot;}}]"><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; justify-content: space-between; align-items: center;"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewBox="0 0 8 2.125" height="16pt">            <g id="button-delete" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(1 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M 1,1 l 3,0 4,4 4,-4 3,0	     0,3 -4,4 4,4 0,3	     -3,0 -4,-4 -4,4 -3,0	     0,-3 4,-4 -4,-4 0,-3 z" fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-fullscreen" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(21 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M  1, 1 l 3,0 1.5,1.5 -3,0 0,3 -1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15, 1 l -3,0 -1.5,1.5 3,0 0,3 1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15,15 l -3,0 -1.5,-1.5 3,0 0,-3 1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  1,15 l 3,0 1.5,-1.5 -3,0 0,-3 -1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  4, 4 l 2,0 1,1 -2,0 0,2 -1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12, 4 l -2,0 -1,1 2,0 0,2 1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12,12 l -2,0 -1,-1 2,0 0,-2 1,1 0,2 z	     M  4,12 l 2,0 1,-1 -2,0 0,-2 -1,1 0,2 z	     " fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-collapse" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(41 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <circle cx="8" cy="8" r="5.375" stroke="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke-width="2.75" fill="none"></circle>            </g>        </svg><input type="text" value="" class="title-input"></div><div class="content"><textarea class="custom-scrollbar" type="text" size="11" style="background-color: rgb(34, 34, 38); transform: scale(0.0273237, 0.0273237); display: none; left: 6764.35px; top: 310.415px;" data-node_json="{&quot;anchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.46905361987859434,&quot;y&quot;:0.013924808283155598},&quot;anchorForce&quot;:0,&quot;mouseAnchor&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0.00007420062161017649,&quot;y&quot;:-0.00039220328565356573},&quot;createdAt&quot;:&quot;2023-05-31T15:14:03.412Z&quot;,&quot;uuid&quot;:&quot;13&quot;,&quot;pos&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:-0.46905361987859434,&quot;y&quot;:0.013924808283155598},&quot;scale&quot;:0.0012892358004760766,&quot;intrinsicScale&quot;:1,&quot;vel&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;force&quot;:{&quot;x&quot;:0,&quot;y&quot;:0},&quot;followingMouse&quot;:0,&quot;removed&quot;:false,&quot;isResizing&quot;:false,&quot;observer&quot;:{}}" data-node_extras="[{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;window&quot;},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,0,1],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;untitled&quot;}},{&quot;f&quot;:&quot;textarea&quot;,&quot;a&quot;:{&quot;p&quot;:[0,1,0],&quot;v&quot;:&quot;---\ntags:\n  - NIPoPoWs\n---\n\n# Light Clients\n\n**Light Clients** are essential when considering the hurdles cryptocurrency faces with mass adoption. Most crypto users do not have the necessary tools to run a full node. Running a full node means having a strong processor, electricity costs, and potentially hundreds of gigabytes of memory to store the entire blockchain. \n\n- NIPoPoWs enable Ergo to build a mobile [SPV](light-spv-node.md) client that only needs around 100KB of block headers to be downloaded. Verifying whether a transaction happened on the blockchain becomes simplified, enabling users to interact with the blockchain using more efficient and convenient mobile wallets.\n- NIPoPoWs can also be used to create a [Pruned Full-Node](pruned-full-node.md) using UTXO Set Snapshots. &quot;}}]"><div class="window"><div style="display: flex; justify-content: space-between; align-items: center;"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" viewBox="0 0 8 2.125" height="16pt">            <g id="button-delete" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(1 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M 1,1 l 3,0 4,4 4,-4 3,0	     0,3 -4,4 4,4 0,3	     -3,0 -4,-4 -4,4 -3,0	     0,-3 4,-4 -4,-4 0,-3 z" fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-fullscreen" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(21 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <path d="M  1, 1 l 3,0 1.5,1.5 -3,0 0,3 -1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15, 1 l -3,0 -1.5,1.5 3,0 0,3 1.5,-1.5 0,-3 z	     M 15,15 l -3,0 -1.5,-1.5 3,0 0,-3 1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  1,15 l 3,0 1.5,-1.5 -3,0 0,-3 -1.5,1.5 0,3 z	     M  4, 4 l 2,0 1,1 -2,0 0,2 -1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12, 4 l -2,0 -1,1 2,0 0,2 1,-1 0,-2 z	     M 12,12 l -2,0 -1,-1 2,0 0,-2 1,1 0,2 z	     M  4,12 l 2,0 1,-1 -2,0 0,-2 -1,1 0,2 z	     " fill="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke="none"></path>            </g>            <g id="button-collapse" transform="scale(0.125 0.125) translate(41 1)">                <rect x="0" y="0" width="16" height="16" fill="none" stroke="none"></rect>                <circle cx="8" cy="8" r="5.375" stroke="RGB(170,170,170)" stroke-width="2.75" fill="none"></circle>            </g>        </svg><input type="text" value="" class="title-input"></div><div class="content"><textarea class="custom-scrollbar" type="text" size="11" style="background-color: rgb(34, 34, 38);
